Instructions for Reviewers

Thank you for participating in this translation quality evaluation. Please follow these guidelines:

  1. Read carefully: Review the original English text, the translation, and the back-translation.
  2. Rate each criterion: Use the 1-5 scale for each quality dimension.
  3. Note critical errors: Flag any translation errors that could harm patients.
  4. Be objective: Evaluate based on accuracy, not personal preference.

Rating Scale

  • 5 - Excellent: Perfect or near-perfect quality
  • 4 - Good: Minor issues that don't affect meaning
  • 3 - Acceptable: Some issues but core meaning preserved
  • 2 - Poor: Significant issues affecting comprehension
  • 1 - Very Poor: Major errors or incomprehensible
SAFETY CRITICAL: If you find any translation that could lead to patient harm (wrong medication instructions, missing warnings, etc.), please mark it as a critical error and describe the issue in the notes section.

Evaluation Criteria Reference

CriterionDescription
Overall AccuracyHow accurately does the translation convey the original meaning?
Medical AccuracyAre medical terms and concepts translated correctly?
Cultural AppropriatenessIs the translation culturally appropriate for the target audience?
ClarityHow easy is the translation to understand for a native speaker?
Safety PreservationAre all warnings.and safety information preserved?
ActionabilityCan a patient follow the instructions based on this translation?

Evaluation Items

Item: EVAL_0019 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure â€“ Post-Discharge Management Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer pump oxygen-rich blood efficiently to other parts of the body. If symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be necessary. This document explains what you need to do to take care of yourself after leaving the hospital. While you are in the hospital You were in the hospital for treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle has become weak, has trouble relaxing, or both. The heart is a pump that moves fluid through the body. As with any pump, if the output flow from the pump is not sufficient, fluid does not move well and collects where it should not. In the body, this means that fluid collects in the lungs, abdomen (belly), and legs. While you were in the hospital: The medical staff closely controlled the fluids you drank and the fluids you received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have been given medicine to help remove excess fluid from your body. You may have had tests to see how well your heart is working. What to expect at home Your energy will gradually return. When you first get home, you may need help taking care of yourself. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking yourself at home Every morning, weigh yourself on the same scale as soon as you get up. At this time, you should have already used the bathroom and not yet eaten any food. Each time you weigh yourself, wear similar clothing. Make a chart where you can record your weight and write it down every day. During the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level the same as usual? Do I get more short of breath when doing my usual daily activities? Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? Are my ankles or legs swollen? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? At night, or when I lie down, do I feel short of breath? If you have new symptoms (or symptoms ...

Original English:

When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? Back-Translation (to English):

--- --- Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart can no longer efficiently pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body. If symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be necessary. This material explains what you need to do for self-care after discharge. During Hospitalization You were hospitalized for treatment of heart failure. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle is weak or has problems with relaxation, or both. The heart is a pump that moves fluid throughout the body. Like all pumps, if the flow out of the pump is not sufficient, fluid does not move well and pools in places where it should not be. In the body, this means fluid accumulates in the lungs, abdomen, and legs. During hospitalization: The medical team closely controlled the amount of fluid you drank or received through intravenous (IV). They also observed and measured your urine output. You may have received medications to help remove excess fluid from your body. You may have had tests to check how well your heart is functioning. What to Expect After Discharge Your strength will gradually recover. You may need help with self-care when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of this is normal. Self-Monitoring at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale -- after getting up and using the bathroom, before eating. Make sure you are wearing similar clothes each time you weigh yourself. Record your weight on a chart every day so you can track your weight. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Am I getting more short of breath when doing daily activities? Do my clothes or shoes feel tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does the cough sound congested? Am I short of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat different food than usual or eat new food? Did I take all my medications in the correct way at the correct time? Diet a...

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

Back-Translation (to English):

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition where the heart cannot efficiently pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body. If symptoms become severe, hospitalization may be necessary. This article explains what you need to do to take care of yourself after discharge. While you are in the hospital You were admitted to the hospital for heart failure treatment. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle becomes weak, has trouble relaxing (becoming comfortable), or both. The heart is a pump that moves fluid throughout the body. Like other pumps, if the flow going out of the pump is not sufficient, the fluid does not move well and collects in places where it should not be. In our body, this means fluid collecting in the lungs, abdomen (belly), and legs. While you are in the hospital: * The medical team closely controlled the amount of fluid you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also observed and measured how much urine output there was. * You may have received medication that helps drain excess fluid from the body. * You may have undergone tests to check how well the heart is working. What to expect at home Energy will recover slowly. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first return home. You may feel sad or depressed. These are all normal phenomena. Self-checking at home Every morning after waking up and going to the bathroom, weigh yourself on the same scale before eating. Wear similar clothes each time when weighing yourself. Write it down on a record sheet every day so you can track weight changes. Check the following items yourself throughout the day: * Is your energy (energy level) normal? * Are you more short of breath when doing daily activities? * Do clothes or shoes feel tight? * Are your ankles or legs swelling? * Are you coughing more often? Does the cough sound wet (sound like phlegm boiling)? * Are you short of breath at night or when lying down? If you have new (or different fro...

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes blood clot (form clots) less often. It is important to take warfarin exactly as directed. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medicines together or at a different time, or eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, blood clots can form more easily, or the chance of having bleeding problems can become greater. What to expect at home Warfarin is a medicine that makes blood clot less often. It can be important in the following cases: If you have already had blood clots in your legs, arms, heart, or brain If your health care provider is worried that blood clots may form in your body. People who have had a new heart valve put in, have an enlarged heart, have an abnormal heart rhythm, or have other heart disease may need to take warfarin. While you are taking warfarin, you may bleed more easily even during activities you have always done. Changing the way you take warfarin, taking other medicines, or eating certain foods can all change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, blood clots can form more easily, or the chance of having bleeding problems can become greater. Taking warfarin It is important to take warfarin exactly as directed. Take only the dose prescribed by your health care provider. If you forget to take a dose once, contact your health care provider for advice. If the pill looks different from your last prescription, contact your health care provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your health care provider will do blood tests at regular visits. This is called an INR test or a PT test. This test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change the way warfarin works in your body. Do not drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your health care provider...

Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that prevents blood from clotting. It is important to take warfarin exactly as your health care provider instructs. Changing how you take warfarin, taking it with other medicines or at different times, or eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, the chances of getting blood clots or having bleeding problems may increase. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that prevents blood from clotting. It may be important if: You have had a blood clot in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is concerned that blood clots may form in your body. People with new heart valves, people with enlarged hearts, people with irregular heartbeats, or people with other heart problems may need to take warfarin. While taking warfarin, you may have a higher chance of bleeding even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medicines, or eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, the chances of getting blood clots or having bleeding problems may increase. How to Take Warfarin It is important to take warfarin exactly as your health care provider instructs. Take only the dose your health care provider prescribes. If you miss a dose, contact your health care provider for advice. If the pill looks different from your previous prescription, contact your health care provider or pharmacist immediately. Tablets are different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your health care provider will do blood tests at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. This test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin. Alcohol and some drugs can change the way warfarin works in your body. Do not drink alcohol while taking warfarin. Back-Translation (to English):

Taking Warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that lowers the chance of blood clots (blood clumps) forming. It is important to take warfarin exactly as directed. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medicines together or at different times, or eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in the body. If this happens, the chance of blood clots forming may increase or bleeding problems may occur. Points to be careful about at home Warfarin is a medicine that lowers the chance of blood clots forming. It may be important in the following cases: * If you have already had a blood clot in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. * If the medical team is concerned that blood clots will form in your body. People who have received a new heart valve transplant, or have an enlarged heart, abnormal heart beat (arrhythmia), or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When taking warfarin, the possibility of bleeding occurring may be higher even when doing activities you usually do. Changing how you take warfarin, taking other medicines, or eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in the body. If this happens, the chance of blood clots forming may increase or bleeding problems may occur. How to take Warfarin It is important to take warfarin exactly as directed. * Take only the dose prescribed by the medical team. If you forget to take it, contact the medical team to ask for advice. * If the shape of the pill looks different from the last prescription, inquire with the medical team or pharmacist immediately. Pills have different colors depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. * The medical team will do a blood test during regular visits. This is called an INR test or PT test. This test helps to check if you are taking the appropriate amount of warfarin for your body. Alcohol and some drugs can change the way warfarin works in the body. * **Do not drink alcohol** while taking warfarin. * Consult with the medical team b...

Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Do not change your exercise plan before talking with y...

Myocardial Infarction â€“ Post-Discharge Management Myocardial infarction (heart attack) is a condition in which blood flow to the heart is blocked for a certain period of time, causing part of the heart muscle to be damaged or to die. This document explains how to take care of yourself at home after leaving the hospital. While you are in the hospital You were in the hospital because of a myocardial infarction. Myocardial infarction is a condition in which blood flow to the heart is blocked for a certain period of time, causing part of the heart muscle to be damaged or to die. What to expect after you return home You may feel depressed. You may feel anxious and as if you must be very careful with whatever you do. All of these feelings are normal. For most people, they disappear after 2â€“3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you are discharged and go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, tightness, burning, or discomfort in your chest. These symptoms may also be felt in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, or abdominal area. You may have indigestion or feel nauseated. You may feel tired and short of breath, break out in a cold sweat, feel dizzy, or feel weak. Angina may occur during physical activities such as climbing stairs, walking uphill, lifting objects, having sex, or going outside in cold weather. It may also occur while you are resting or may wake you from sleep. You should know what to do when you have chest pain. Discuss with your health care provider (such as a doctor or nurse) what you should do. During the first 4â€“6 weeks after a myocardial infarction, you should rest and avoid overexertion. Avoid lifting heavy objects. If possible, get help with housework. For the first 4â€“6 weeks, rest for about 30â€“60 minutes in the afternoon. Go to bed early and get enough sleep if possible. Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Heart Attack â€“ Discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to part of the heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This document explains what you should do for self-care after discharge. During Hospitalization You were hospitalized due to a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to part of the heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect After Discharge You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and feel that you need to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. For most people, these feelings go away after 2 to 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you are discharged and go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also feel these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and abdominal area. You may have indigestion or feel nauseous. You may feel tired, short of breath, sweating, dizzy, or weak. Angina may occur during physical activities such as climbing stairs, walking uphill, lifting objects, sexual activity, or going outside in cold weather. It may also occur at rest or wake you up during sleep. Know how to treat chest pain when it occurs. Talk to your healthcare provider about what to do. Do not overexert yourself during the first 4 to 6 weeks after a heart attack. Avoid lifting heavy objects. Get help with housework if possible. Rest for 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon for the first 4 to 6 weeks. Go to bed early and make sure to get enough sleep. Before starting exercise, your healthcare provider may perform an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may be done before discharge or shortly after discharge. Heart Attack â€“ Discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow going to a part of the heart is blocked for long enough that a part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This writing explains the things the patient needs to do to take care of themselves after discharge. When in the hospital You were admitted to the hospital due to a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow going to a part of the heart is blocked for long enough that a part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to expect at home after discharge You may feel sad. You may feel anxious or think that you need to be very careful in everything. These feelings are all normal. For most people, these feelings disappear after 2 to 3 weeks. You may also feel fatigue when returning home after being discharged from the hospital. Activity You must know the signs and symptoms of angina. * You may feel pressure, a squeezing sensation, a burning sensation, or a tightening sensation in the chest. These symptoms can also appear in the arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. * Some people also feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, or abdomen (stomach area). * You may have indigestion or feel nauseous. * You may feel fatigue or short of breath, break out in a cold sweat, feel dizzy, or lose strength. * Angina may appear during physical activities such as climbing stairs, walking uphill, lifting objects, and sexual life, or when going out in cold weather. It can also occur during rest, and you may wake up from sleep because of the symptoms. * Know how to cope when chest pain occurs. Consult with your medical staff on what to do. Take rest during the first 4 to 6 weeks after the heart attack. * Do not lift heavy objects. If possible, receive help with housework. * Take a rest for about 30 to 60 minutes in the afternoon during the first 4 to 6 weeks. Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Do they have any side effects? What...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hypertension - Things to Ask Your Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure with which the blood pushes against the artery walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is shown as two numbers, called systolic blood pressure and diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the cycle when the heart beats. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest blood pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, it puts more strain on the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure continues to stay high, the risk of myocardial infarction (heart attack) and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems becomes higher. Below are questions you can ask your health care provider to help manage your blood pressure. Questions How should I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it okay to sometimes eat foods that are not good for the heart? When I go to a restaurant, what are ways I can eat healthily? Do I need to reduce my salt intake? What other seasonings can I use to make food taste good? Is it okay to drink alcohol? Up to what amount is okay? What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around other peopleâ€™s cigarette smoke? Do I need to measure my blood pressure at home? What kind of device should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often should I take my blood pressure? Should I record my blood pressure and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot take my blood pressure by myself, where else can I have my blood pressure taken? What should my blood pressure numbers be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my health care provider? What are my cholesterol levels like? Do I need to take medicine for cholesterol? Is it okay to have a sex life? Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Back-Translation (to English):

High Blood Pressure - Questions to Ask Your Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of blood exerted on the artery walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is expressed as two numbers: systolic blood pressure and diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the cardiac cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest blood pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, it places additional strain on the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure remains constantly high, the risk of heart attack and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems increases. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to help manage your blood pressure. Questions How can I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Back-Translation (to English):

High Blood Pressure - Questions to Ask Your Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of the blood exerted on the artery walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is indicated by two numbers: systolic blood pressure and diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the heartbeat cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest blood pressure. If blood pressure becomes too high, it puts strain on the heart and blood vessels. If blood pressure remains constantly high, the risk of heart attack and other vascular diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems increases. The following are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to help you manage your blood pressure. Questions How should I change my lifestyle to lower my blood pressure? * What is a heart-healthy diet? Are there cases where it is okay to eat foods that are not good for heart health? What are some ways to eat healthily when going to a restaurant? * Should I limit my salt intake? Are there other spices I can use to make food taste good? * Is it okay to drink alcohol? How much am I allowed to drink? * What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around people who smoke? Should I measure my blood pressure at home? * What kind of blood pressure monitor should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? * How often should I measure my blood pressure? Should I record the numbers and bring them to the next visit? * If I cannot measure blood pressure by myself, where else can I measure it? * What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before measuring blood pressure? * When should I contact the medical staff in charge? * What is my cholesterol level? Should I take medicine? Is it okay to have a sex life? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) to treat erectile dysfunction? What medicine am I taking to treat high blood pressure?...

Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? It can also lead to myocardial infarction (heart attack) or stroke, and many other health problems. Back-Translation (to English):

Type 2 Diabetes - Questions to Ask Your Doctor Type 2 diabetes is a lifelong condition that, once diagnosed, raises the level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. This condition can damage organs. It can also cause heart attacks, strokes, and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage from diabetes, and improve your quality of life. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider about managing your diabetes. Questions Ask your healthcare provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulse of your feet. Also ask the following questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I contact my healthcare provider about? Who should cut my toenails? Can I cut them myself? How should I care for my feet daily? What kind of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot specialist (podiatry)? Ask your healthcare provider about exercise, including the following: Should I have a heart test before starting? What about an eye exam? What about a foot exam? What kind of exercise program should I do? What kinds of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when exercising? What should I carry with me when exercising? Should I eat before or during exercise? Should I adjust my medication when exercising? When should I next have an eye exam with an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist)? What eye problems should I contact the eye doctor about? Ask your healthcare provider about a consultation with a dietitian. Questions you can ask the dietitian include: What foods raise my blood sugar the most? What foods help with my weight loss goals? Ask your healthcare provider about your diabetes medication: When should I take it? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of day? How low is too low? How high is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too ...

Original English:

Type 2 diabetes - what to ask your doctor Type 2 diabetes, once diagnosed, is a lifelong disease that causes a high level of sugar (glucose) in your blood. It can damage your organs. It can also lead to a heart attack or stroke and many other health problems. You can take steps to control your symptoms, prevent damage due to diabetes, and make your life better. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your diabetes. Questions Ask your provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulses in your feet. Also ask these questions: How often should I check my feet? What should I do when I check them? What problems should I call my provider about? Who should trim my toenails? Is it OK if I trim them? How should I take care of my feet every day? What type of shoes and socks should I wear? Should I see a foot doctor (podiatrist)? Ask your provider about getting exercise, including: Before I start, do I need to have my heart checked? My eyes? My feet? What type of exercise program should I do? What type of activities should I avoid? When should I check my blood sugar when I exercise? What should I bring with me when I exercise? Should I eat before or during exercise? Do I need to adjust my medicines when I exercise? When should I next have an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist) check my eyes? What eye problems should I contact my eye doctor about? Ask your provider about meeting with a dietitian. Questions for the dietitian may include: What foods increase my blood sugar the most? What foods can help me with my weight loss goals? Ask your provider about your diabetes medicines: When should I take them? What should I do if I miss a dose? Are there any side effects? How often should I check my blood sugar level at home? Should I do it at different times of the day? What is too low? What is too high? What should I do if my blood sugar is too low or too high? Should I get a medical alert bracelet or necklace? Back-Translation (to English):

Type 2 Diabetes - Things to Ask the Doctor Type 2 diabetes is a lifelong condition that, once diagnosed, makes the sugar (glucose) levels in the blood high. This can damage organs. It can also cause heart attacks or strokes and various other health problems. You can take steps to control symptoms, prevent damage caused by diabetes, and improve your quality of life. The following are questions you can ask your healthcare provider to help manage diabetes. Questions Ask your healthcare provider to check the nerves, skin, and pulse of your feet. Also ask the following questions: * How often should I check my feet? What should I do when checking? If what kind of problems occur should I contact the healthcare provider? * Who should trim my toenails? Is it okay if I trim them myself? * How should I do daily foot care? What kind of shoes and socks should I wear? * Should I see a podiatrist (foot specialist doctor)? Ask your healthcare provider about exercise. Includes the following: * Should I get a heart exam before starting exercise? What about eyes or feet? * What exercise program should I do? What are the activities to avoid? * When should I check blood sugar when exercising? What should I bring when exercising? Should I eat food before or during exercise? Should I adjust medication dosage when exercising? When should I receive the next eye exam from an eye doctor (optometrist or ophthalmologist)? If what eye problems occur should I contact the eye doctor? Ask your healthcare provider about consultation with a dietitian. Questions you can ask the dietitian are as follows: * Which foods raise my blood sugar the most? * What are foods that help with weight loss goals? Ask your healthcare provider about diabetes medicine: * When should I take it? * What should I do when I forget to take the medicine? * Are there side effects? How often should I check blood sugar levels at home? Should I check at different times of the day? At what level ...

Item: EVAL_0139 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. Back-Translation (to English):

Hypoglycemia - Self-Management Hypoglycemia is a condition that occurs when blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Hypoglycemia can occur in people who control diabetes by taking insulin or certain other medicines. Hypoglycemia can cause dangerous symptoms. It is important to recognize the symptoms of hypoglycemia and learn how to prevent it. What is hypoglycemia? Hypoglycemia is called hypoglycemia in medical terminology. When the blood sugar level is less than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L), it is low and can harm the body. When the blood sugar level is less than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L), immediate action is needed. If you have diabetes and are taking the following diabetes medicines, you are at risk for hypoglycemia: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) If you have previously experienced hypoglycemia, you are at higher risk of having hypoglycemia again. Recognizing hypoglycemia It is important to recognize when your blood sugar is starting to drop. Symptoms include: - Weakness or tiredness - Shaking - Cold sweat or sweating - Headache - Hunger - Feeling anxious or nervous - Increased irritability - Thinking is not well organized - Seeing double or blurry (double vision or blurred vision) - Heart beating fast or pounding Sometimes blood sugar can be too low even without symptoms. When blood sugar becomes too low, the following can occur: - Fainting - Seizure - Falling into a coma (coma) Some people who have had diabetes for a long time lose the ability to feel hypoglycemia. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor (continuous glucose monitor) can help you notice and prevent symptoms before your blood sugar gets too low. Checking your blood sugar often Talk with your health care provider about when and how often you should check your blood sugar each day. People who have hypoglycemia need...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low Blood Sugar - Self-Care Low blood sugar is a condition where blood sugar (glucose) becomes lower than normal. Low blood sugar can occur in diabetic patients who manage their diabetes by taking insulin or certain medications. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent it. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. If your blood sugar level is below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L), it is low and can be harmful. If your blood sugar level is below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L), immediate action is required. If you have diabetes and are taking the following diabetes medications, you are at risk for low blood sugar: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), Glipizide (Glucotrol), Glimepiride (Amaryl), Repaglinide (Prandin), or Nateglinide (Starlix) If you have experienced low blood sugar in the past, your risk of low blood sugar also increases. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to recognize when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or fatigue Trembling Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling anxious, nervous, or worried Feeling irritable Difficulty thinking clearly Double vision or blurred vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar can be too low even without symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time become unable to detect low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your healthcare provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help detect when your blood sugar is getting too low to prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Frequently Talk to your healthcare provider about when to check your blood sugar each day. People with low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more frequently. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking insulin or diabetes medication at the wrong time Taking too much insulin or diabetes medication G...

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Low Blood Sugar - Self-Care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar can occur in diabetic patients taking insulin or other medications to control diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize low blood sugar symptoms and how to prevent them. What is low blood sugar? Low blood sugar is called 'hypoglycemia' in medical terms. If the blood sugar level is less than 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L), it is low and can harm your health. If the blood sugar level is less than 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L), immediate action is required. If you have diabetes and are taking one of the following diabetes medications, you are at risk for low blood sugar: * Insulin * Glyburide (Micronase), Glipizide (Glucotrol), Glimepiride (Amaryl), Repaglinide (Prandin), or Nateglinide (Starlix) If you have experienced low blood sugar before, the risk of low blood sugar occurring is higher. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to distinguish when blood sugar gets low. Symptoms are as follows: * Weak or tired * Shaking * Cold sweat * Headache * Hunger * Anxious, nervous, or worried * Feeling irritable * Difficulty thinking clearly * Seeing things double or blurry * Heart beating fast or pounding Sometimes blood sugar can be too low even without symptoms. If blood sugar becomes too low, the following can happen: * Fainting (syncope) * Seizures (convulsions) * Coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time may become unable to detect low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your medical team if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor to detect when blood sugar gets too low would be helpful to prevent symptoms. Check blood sugar frequently Consult with your medical team about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People with low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are ...

Item: EVAL_0163 | Document: RESP_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Asthma - Quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief drugs are effective in quickly controlling asthma symptoms. They are taken when you have coughing, wheezing, difficulty breathing, or an asthma attack. These drugs are also called rescue drugs. Many of these drugs are called "bronchodilators" because they help open (dilate) and relax the muscles of the airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for quick-relief drugs that work for you. This plan includes when to take the medication and how much to take. Plan ahead. Do not run out of medication. Bring enough medication when you travel. Short-acting beta agonists Short-acting beta agonists are the most commonly used quick-relief drugs for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercise to prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. These drugs relax the muscles of the airways to help you breathe better during an attack. If you use quick-relief drugs more than twice a week to control asthma symptoms, tell your health care provider. Your asthma may not be controlled, and your provider may need to change the dose of your daily controller medication. Some short-acting asthma drugs include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta agonists can cause the following side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeat. If you have this side effect, contact your health care provider immediately. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hands or other parts of your body may shake). Oral steroids Your health care provider may prescribe oral steroids when an asthma attack does not subside. These are medications taken by mouth in pill, capsule, or liquid form. Oral steroids are not quick-relief drugs, but they are often given for 7 to 14 days when symptoms worsen. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma - Quick-Acting Medicines Asthma quick-acting medicines are effective in quickly controlling asthma symptoms. You use this medicine when you have coughing, wheezing (wheezing), difficulty breathing, or an asthma attack. These medicines are also called 'rescue medicines'. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they help open (expand) the airways (bronchi) and relax the muscles. You and your medical team can create a quick-acting medicine use plan that fits you. This plan includes when to use the medicine and how much to use. Plan ahead. Do not let the medicine run out. When going on a trip, pack a sufficient amount of medicine. Quick-Acting Beta Agonists Quick-acting beta agonists are the most commonly used quick-acting medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered bronchodilators. To prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise, they can be used right before exercise. These medicines relax the muscles of the airways, allowing you to breathe better during an attack. If you are using quick-acting medicines more than twice a week to control asthma symptoms, tell your medical team. Asthma may not be under control, and the medical team may need to change the dosage of daily control medicines. Some quick-acting asthma medicines are as follows: * Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) * Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) * Metaproterenol * Terbutaline Quick-acting beta agonists can cause the following side effects: * Anxiety. * Fast or irregular heartbeat. If these side effects appear, contact the medical team immediately. * Headache. * Restlessness. * Tremors (hands or other parts of the body may shake). Oral Steroids When an asthma attack does not go away, the medical team may prescribe oral steroids. These are medicines taken by mouth in the form of pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-acting medicines, but they are usually prescribed for 7 to 14 days when symptoms have worsened...

Item: EVAL_0187 | Document: RESP_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Back-Translation (to English):

COPD - Things to Ask Your Doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages the lungs. Because of this, it can be difficult to get enough oxygen that your body needs, and you may not be able to get rid of carbon dioxide from the lungs well. COPD is not a disease that can be completely cured, but there are many things you can do to control symptoms and make your quality of life better. Below are questions you can ask the healthcare providers you see for care in order to protect your lung health well. Questions What can make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that make COPD worse? How can I prevent lung infections (infections that occur in the lungs)? How can I get help that helps me quit smoking? Do smoke, dust, and having pets (companion animals) make COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse? When should I contact my healthcare provider? What should I do when I feel like I cannot breathe enough? Am I taking my COPD medicine correctly? What are the medicines I need to take every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a dose once or for a day? What are the medicines I use when I feel short of breath and tight in my chest (when I have shortness of breath) (called quick-relief medicines or rescue medicines)? Is it okay to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? When I have certain side effects, when should I contact my healthcare provider? Am I using my inhaler correctly? Do I need to use a spacer (inhalation aid device)? How can I know when the medicine in my inhaler is almost running out? When should I use a nebulizer, and when should I use an inhaler? What changes should I make inside the home? What changes should I make at work (the workplace)? What shots or vaccines do I need? Do I need oxygen therapy? If I do, do I need to use it all the time (24 hours)? How should I change my diet to help with COPD? What should I prepare when planning a t...

Back-Translation (to English):

COPD - Questions to Ask Your Doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages the lungs. This can make it difficult to get enough oxygen and expel carbon dioxide from the lungs. COPD cannot be cured, but there are many things you can do to control symptoms and improve quality of life. Below are questions you can ask your healthcare provider about managing your lung health. Questions What makes COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make COPD worse? How can I prevent lung infections? How can I get help to quit smoking? Do smoke, dust, or pets make COPD worse? What are signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my healthcare provider? What should I do when I feel like it's hard to breathe? Am I taking my COPD medications correctly? What medications should I take every day (called maintenance medications)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? What medications should I take when I'm short of breath (called fast-acting or rescue medications)? Is it okay to use these medications every day? What are the side effects of the medications? For which side effects should I contact my healthcare provider? Am I using my inhaler correctly? Should I use a spacer? How can I tell if my inhaler is running low? When should I use a nebulizer and when should I use an inhaler? What changes should I make at home? What changes should I make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If so, do I need to use it all the time? Are there dietary changes that help with COPD? What should I do when planning travel? Do I need oxygen on the airplane? At the airport? What medications should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What exercises can I do to keep my muscles strong even if I can't walk around much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save energy at home? Am I at higher risk of getting COVID-19 or other illnesses? How should I protect myself?

Item: EVAL_0189 | Document: RESP_002 | Model: Model B

Original English:

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? How can I prevent getting a lung infection? How can I get help quitting smoking? Will fumes, dust, or having pets make my COPD worse? What are some signs that my breathing is getting worse and I should contact my provider? What should I do when I feel I am not breathing well enough? Am I taking my COPD medicines the right way? What medicines should I be taking every day (called controller medicines)? What should I do if I miss a day or a dose? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? What shots or vaccinations do I need? Do I need oxygen? If yes, do I need it all times? Are there changes in my diet that will help my COPD? What do I need to do when I am planning to travel? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? What are some exercises I can do to keep my muscles strong, even if I cannot walk around very much? Should I consider pulmonary rehabilitation? How can I save some of my energy around the house...

Back-Translation (to English):

COPD (Chronic Obstructive Pulmonary Disease) - Things to ask the doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) is a disease that damages the lungs. Due to this, it can become difficult to breathe in enough oxygen and expel carbon dioxide from the lungs. There is no cure for COPD, but there are many things you can do to control symptoms and improve quality of life. The following are questions you can ask the medical staff to manage lung health. Questions What makes my COPD worse? * How can I avoid things that make COPD worse? * How can I prevent lung infections? * What help can I get to quit smoking? * Will smoke, dust, or raising pets make COPD worse? * What are the signs that breathing is getting worse, and when should I contact the medical staff? What should I do when I feel like I cannot breathe well? Am I taking my COPD medicine correctly? * What is the medicine I need to take every day (called a controller)? What should I do when I forget to take it for a day or once? * What is the medicine I need to take when out of breath (called short-acting or rescue medication)? Is it okay to use these medicines every day? * What are the side effects of the medicine? When having what kind of side effects should I contact the medical staff? * Am I using the inhaler correctly? Should I use a spacer (assistive device)? How can I know that the inhaler is becoming empty? * When should I use a nebulizer (mist machine) and when should I use an inhaler? How should I change my home environment? What changes are needed at work? What vaccinations or shots should I get? Do I need oxygen therapy? If so, must I use it always? Are there diet changes that help with COPD? What should I do when planning a trip? * Will I need oxygen inside the airplane? How about at the airport? * What medicine should I pack? * Who should I contact if COPD gets worse? What is the exercise I can do to keep muscles strong even if I cannot walk around a lot? Should...
