Item: EVAL_0211 | Document: MED_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They are used to treat problems of the heart, blood vessels, and kidneys. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. This medicine lowers blood pressure and helps the heart work with less effort. In this way, it can prevent some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most patients with heart failure take this medicine or a similar medicine. These medicines treat high blood pressure, stroke, or myocardial infarction (heart attack). They may help lower the risk of having a stroke or heart attack. These medicines are also used to treat kidney problems. Especially if you have diabetes, they may help prevent the kidneys from getting worse or help improve kidney function. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider (such as a doctor or nurse) whether you should take this medicine. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most have similar effects. However, the side effects can be different for each medicine. How to take ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all medicines exactly as your healthcare provider instructs. See your healthcare provider regularly for checkups. Your healthcare provider will measure your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicine is working well. Your healthcare provider may sometimes change the dose of the medicine. Also, follow these instructions: Take the medicine at the same time every day. Do not stop taking the medicine without talking to your healthcare provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. When you travel, take enough medicine with you. Talk to your healthcare provider before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin. Tell your healthcare provider about any other medicines you are taking, such as over-the-counter medicines, diuretics (water pills, water medicine), potassium su...

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE Inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medications. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE Inhibitors Help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. This medication makes the heart work less hard by lowering blood pressure. This prevents some types of heart disease from getting worse. Most people with heart failure take this medication or a similar medication. This medication treats high blood pressure, stroke, or heart attack. It can help lower the risk of stroke or heart attack. It is also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent kidneys from getting worse or even improve kidney function, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider if you should take this medication. Types of ACE Inhibitors There are many names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work similarly. Side effects can vary by medication. How to Take ACE Inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all medications as directed by your healthcare provider. Have regular follow-up appointments with your healthcare provider. Your healthcare provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medication is working properly. Your healthcare provider may change your dose from time to time. Also: Try to take your medication at the same time every day. Do not stop taking your medication without talking to your healthcare provider first. Plan ahead so you don't run out of medication. Bring enough when you travel. Talk to your healthcare provider before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin. Tell your healthcare provider about other medications you are currently taking, including those purchased without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbs and dietary supplements. Do not take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. If you become pregnant while taking this...

Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. This medicine treats heart, blood vessel, and kidney (kidney) problems. **How ACE inhibitors help** ACE inhibitors are used for the treatment of heart disease and high blood pressure. This medicine lowers blood pressure so that the heart does not work too hard. This prevents certain heart diseases from getting worse. Most people with heart failure take this medicine or a similar medicine. This medicine treats high blood pressure, stroke, or heart attack. Also, it can help lower the risk of stroke or heart attack. Also, it is used to treat kidney problems. Especially if you have diabetes, it can help prevent the kidney condition from getting worse or improve kidney function. If you have these problems, ask your healthcare provider if you should take this medicine. **Types of ACE inhibitors** ACE inhibitors have various names and brands. Most effects are similar. Side effects may differ for each medicine. **How to take ACE inhibitors** ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all medicines as directed by your healthcare provider. Receive medical treatment regularly. Your healthcare provider will measure blood pressure and do blood tests to check if the medicine is working properly. Sometimes they may change the dosage. Also: * Try to take the medicine at the same time every day. * Do not stop taking the medicine without first consulting your healthcare provider. * Prepare in advance so that you do not run out of medicine. When traveling, pack enough medicine. * Consult your healthcare provider before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin. * Inform your healthcare provider of other medications you are currently taking, including medicines purchased without a prescription, diuretics, potassium supplements, herbal medicines, or health supplements. * Do not take ACE inhibitors if you are planning pregnancy, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Item: EVAL_0235 | Document: EMER_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Post-discharge care You were admitted to the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke occurs when the blood flow to a part of the brain stops. At home, follow your healthcare providerâ€™s self-care instructions. The information below is to help you remember. While you were in the hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain and to help your heart, lungs, and other important organs recover. After your condition became stable, the medical staff performed tests and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and to prevent future strokes. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps with recovery after a stroke. What to expect at home Because of brain damage from the stroke, you may feel the following problems: - Changes in behavior - Ability to do simple tasks - Memory - Moving one side of the body - Muscle spasms - Concentration - Sensation or awareness of a part of the body - Swallowing - Speaking or understanding what others say - Thinking - Visual disturbance where you can see only one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to help you regain these functions. After a stroke, depression is relatively common as you go through the process of adapting to changes. This can occur at any time from right after the stroke up to as long as 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive a car without your healthcare providerâ€™s permission. Moving After a stroke, it may be difficult to move or to do the things you used to do. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your healthcare provider, therapist, or nurse how you can change your home to make daily activities easier. Learn how to prevent falls and how to use the bathroom safely. Family and caregivers may need to help with the following: - Exercises to prevent the elbows, shoulders, and other joints from becoming stiff - Watching...

Original English:

Stroke - discharge You were in the hospital after having a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home follow your health care provider's instructions on self-care. Use the information below as a reminder. When You're in the Hospital First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain, and to help the heart, lungs, and other important organs heal. After you were stable, your providers did testing and started treatment to help you recover from the stroke and prevent a future stroke. You may have stayed in a special unit that helps people recover after a stroke. What to Expect at Home Because of possible injury to the brain from the stroke, you may notice problems with: Changes in behavior Doing easy tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Paying attention Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Talking or understanding others Thinking Seeing to one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you used to do alone before the stroke. You may have physical or occupational therapy to restore your ability to perform these functions. Depression after a stroke is fairly common as you learn to live with the changes. It may develop soon after the stroke or up to 2 years after the stroke. Do not drive your car without your provider's permission. Moving Around Moving around and doing normal tasks may be hard after a stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your provider, therapist, or nurse about making changes in your home to make it easier to do everyday activities. Find out about what you can do to prevent falls and keep your bathroom safe to use. Family and caregivers may need to help with: Exercises to keep your elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose Watching for joint tightening (contractures) Making sure splints are used in the correct way Making sure arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lying If you or your loved one is using a wheelchair, fol...

Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Discharge You were hospitalized after experiencing a stroke. A stroke occurs when blood flow to a part of the brain stops. At home, follow the self-care instructions from your healthcare provider. Use the information below for reference. During hospitalization First, you received treatment to prevent further damage to the brain and to help the heart, lungs and other vital organs recover. After your condition stabilized, your healthcare provider conducted tests and began treatment for stroke recovery and prevention of future strokes. You may have been admitted to a specialized unit that helps with recovery after stroke. What to expect at home Brain damage from stroke may cause you to experience the following problems: Behavior changes Performing simple tasks Memory Moving one side of the body Muscle spasms Concentration Sensation or awareness of one part of the body Swallowing Speaking or understanding others Thinking Vision loss on one side (hemianopia) You may need help with daily activities that you did alone before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to regain these functions. It is quite common to develop depression while adjusting to life changed after stroke. Back-Translation (to English):

Stroke - Discharge You were admitted to the hospital for a stroke. A stroke happens when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home, follow your medical team's self-care instructions. Use the information below as a reminder. **When You Were in the Hospital** First, you received treatment to stop further damage to the brain and help the heart, lungs, and other vital organs heal. After your condition stabilized, the medical team performed tests and started treatment for stroke recovery and prevention of future strokes. You may have stayed in a special unit (stroke intensive care unit) that helps recovery after a stroke. **What to Expect at Home** Because of possible brain damage from the stroke, you may experience the following problems: * Behavior changes * Doing simple tasks * Memory * Moving one side of the body * Muscle spasms * Paying attention * Sensation or awareness of body parts * Swallowing * Speaking or understanding others * Thinking skills (thinking) * Seeing only one side (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you did alone before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to recover the ability to perform these functions. Depression after stroke is quite common as you adjust to a changed life. It can occur immediately after the stroke or up to 2 years later. **Do not drive without your medical team's permission.** **Moving Around** After a stroke, moving or doing normal tasks can be difficult. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your medical team, therapist, or nurse about changing your home environment to make daily activities easier. Learn how to prevent falls and use the bathroom safely. Family and caregivers may need to help with the following: * Exercises to keep elbows, shoulders, and other joints loose * Watching for joint stiffening (contracture) * Checking that splints are used correctly * Checking that arms and legs are in a good position when sitting or lyin...

Item: EVAL_0259 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical Wound Care â€“ Open Wound An incision is a cut that opens the skin during surgery. This is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and some incisions are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery you had. Sometimes the incision site can open up. This may be the entire incision or only part of it. In such cases, the surgeon in charge may decide not to close the wound again with sutures (stitches). What to expect at home If the surgeon decided not to close the wound again with sutures, you will need to care for the wound at home because it may take time for the wound to heal. The wound heals from the bottom toward the top. The dressing helps absorb drainage and helps prevent the skin from closing before the inside of the wound has filled in. Proper handwashing It is important to wash your hands well before changing the dressing. You may use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you may wash your hands by following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands under warm running water with your hands pointing down. Apply soap and rub your hands together for 15â€“30 seconds (about the time it takes to sing the â€œHappy Birthdayâ€ song or the â€œAlphabet Songâ€ once). Clean well under your fingernails. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the old dressing Your health care provider (doctor or nurse) will tell you how often to change the dressing. When you are getting ready to change the dressing, follow these steps: Wash your hands well before touching the dressing. Make sure all the supplies you need are close by. Prepare a clean work area. Remove the old dressing. Carefully remove the tape from your skin. Put on clean (not sterile) medical gloves and gently pull off the old dressing. If the dressing is stuck to the wound, moisten it with water and try again. However, this does not apply if your health care provider instructed you to remove it dry. Item: EVAL_0260 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model A

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Surgical Wound Care - Open Wound An incision refers to cutting the skin during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, and some are long. The size of the incision depends on the type of surgery received. Sometimes the incision site may open up. This can occur in the entire incision or in a portion of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). Care at Home If your surgeon did not close the wound again with sutures, healing may take time, so you need to care for it at home. The wound heals from bottom to top. Dressings serve to absorb discharge and prevent the skin from closing before the wound underneath is filled in. Proper Hand Washing It is important to wash your hands thoroughly before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands following these steps: Remove all jewelry from your hands. Wet your hands with warm running water, pointing hands downward. Apply soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (about the time it takes to sing "Happy Birthday" or the "ABC Song" once). Clean under your fingernails as well. Rinse thoroughly. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your healthcare provider will tell you how often to change the dressing. How to prepare for dressing change: Wash your hands thoroughly before touching the dressing. Make sure all necessary supplies are within reach. Have a clean work area. How to remove the old dressing: Carefully peel the tape from the skin. Using clean (not sterile) medical gloves, grasp and remove the old dressing. If the dressing is stuck to the wound, moisten it and try again. The exception is if your healthcare provider instructed you to remove it dry. Place the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Wash your hands again after removing the old dressing. Wound Care You can use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around the wound: Use normal saline (salt water) or mild soapy wa...

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

Open Surgical Wound Care An incision refers to cutting the skin during surgery. This is also called a surgical wound. The incision site can be small or long. The size of the incision site depends on the type of surgery received. Sometimes, the incision site opens up. The entire incision site may open, or only a part of it may open. Your attending surgeon may decide not to close this again with sutures (stitches). ### Care at Home If the surgeon does not close the wound again with sutures, you must care for it at home because it may take time for the wound to heal. The wound will heal by filling up from the inside (bottom). The dressing absorbs drainage and prevents the skin from closing before the inside of the wound is filled. ### Proper Hand Washing It is important to wash your hands clean before changing the dressing. You can use an alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or, wash your hands according to the following steps. 1. Remove all jewelry from your hands. 2. Wet your hands under warm running water with your fingertips pointing down. 3. Apply soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (about the time it takes to sing the 'Happy Birthday' song or the 'Alphabet Song' once). Wash clean under your fingernails as well. 4. Rinse clean with water. 5. Wipe dry with a clean towel. ### Removing the Old Dressing The medical staff will tell you the number of times to change the dressing. To prepare for dressing change: * Wash your hands before touching the dressing. * Check that all necessary supplies are ready. * Clean the space where you will work. Removing old dressing: 1. Carefully peel off the tape attached to the skin. 2. Put on clean (does not need to be sterile) medical gloves and grab and peel off the old dressing. 3. If the dressing is stuck to the wound, wet it with water and try again, unless the medical staff instructed you to pull it off dry. 4. Put the removed dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. 5. Wash your hands again after r...

Item: EVAL_0283 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model C

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge Instructions You have had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint (prosthesis). This document tells you how to take care of your new hip after you leave the hospital. While you are in the hospital You have had hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to expect at home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches with little help. Most people do not need a walker or crutches after 2 to 4 weeks. Be sure to talk with your orthopedic surgeon about how long you need to use crutches. You should be able to get dressed with only a little help, and you should be able to get in and out of a bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the bathroom without much help. For several months after surgery, you must be very careful so that the artificial hip does not come out (dislocate). You will learn exercises to strengthen your new hip and you must follow special precautions. For 1 to 2 weeks after discharge, someone should always be with you at home. You will need someone to help with preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to an activity level similar to before. However, you should avoid strenuous contact sports such as downhill skiing or soccer and American football. Instead, you can do exercises that put less strain on the joints, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Preparing your home environment Your bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge. At the same time, it should be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the bed. A hospital bed is not absolutely necessary, but the mattress should be firm. Remove anything in the home that could cause you to fall. Learn how to prevent falls...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement - Discharge You have had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This document will tell you what you need to do to care for your new hip after you are discharged. When You Were in the Hospital You had a hip replacement, which replaced all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect After Discharge By the time you are discharged, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without much help. Most people do not need a walker or crutches after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when you should stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help, and you should be able to get in and out of bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the bathroom without much help. You will need to be careful not to dislocate your artificial hip, especially during the first few months after surgery. You will learn exercises to strengthen your new hip and must follow special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehabilitation center. You will need help with meal preparation, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous activity level. You should avoid contact sports such as skiing or soccer, and football. However, you should be able to do low-impact activities such as hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Preparing Your Home Environment Your bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge of the bed. A hospital bed may not be necessary, but the mattress should be firm. Remove tripping hazards from your home. Learn how to prevent falls. Clear loose wires or cords from walkw...

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement - Discharge You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint (hip joint) with an artificial joint (prosthesis). This article tells you what you need to do to manage your new hip joint after leaving the hospital. While in the hospital You received hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is also called a prosthesis. Life at home after discharge By the time you go home, you should be able to walk using a walker or crutches without much help. Most people do not need these after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon (the doctor who operated) about when to stop using crutches. Also, you should be able to dress with only a little help, and be able to sit and stand up from a bed or chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. Especially during the first few months after surgery, you must be careful so that the artificial hip joint does not dislocate (fall out). You must learn exercises to make the new hip joint strong and take special precautions. You should be with someone at home for 1 to 2 weeks after being discharged from the hospital or rehabilitation center. You need someone to help with meal preparation, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you will be able to return to your previous level of activity. You should avoid contact sports such as downhill skiing, American football, and soccer. However, you can do low-impact activities such as hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Setting up the home environment The bed should be low enough so that your feet touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Also, the bed should be high enough so that your hips are positioned higher than your knees when you sit on the edge of the bed. You will not need a hospital bed, but a firm mattress is good. Remove objects in the house that have a risk of tripping you. Learn how to preven...

Item: EVAL_0295 | Document: CARD_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Heart Failure - Discharge Heart failure is a condition in which the heart is no longer able to pump oxygen-rich blood to the rest of the body efficiently. When symptoms become severe, a hospital stay may be necessary. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital to have your heart failure treated. Heart failure occurs when the muscles of your heart are weak or have trouble relaxing, or both. Your heart is a pump that moves fluids through your body. As with any pump, if the flow out of the pump is not enough, fluids do not move well and they get stuck in places they should not be. In your body, this means that fluid collects in your lungs, abdomen, and legs. While you were in the hospital: Your health care team closely adjusted the fluids you drank or received through an intravenous (IV) line. They also watched and measured how much urine you produced. You may have received medicines to help your body get rid of extra fluids. You may have had tests to check how well your heart was working. What to Expect at Home Your energy will slowly return. You may need help taking care of yourself when you first get home. You may feel sad or depressed. All of these things are normal. Checking Yourself at Home Weigh yourself every morning on the same scale when you get up -- before you eat but after you use the bathroom. Make sure you are wearing similar clothing each time you weigh yourself. Write down your weight every day on a chart so that you can keep track of it. Throughout the day, ask yourself: Is my energy level normal? Do I get more short of breath when I am doing my everyday activities? Are my clothes or shoes feeling tight? Are my ankles or legs swelling? Am I coughing more often? Does my cough sound wet? Do I get short of breath at night or when I lie down? If you are having new (or different) symptoms, ask yourself: Did I eat something different than usua...

This document explains what you must do to take care of yourself when leaving the hospital. When in the Hospital You were hospitalized for heart failure treatment. Heart failure occurs when the heart muscle is weak or there is a problem with relaxation, or both. The heart is a pump that moves fluid through the body. Item: EVAL_0303 | Document: CARD_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Taking warfarin Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines with it or at different times, and eating certain foods can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. What to Expect at Home Warfarin is a medicine that makes your blood less likely to form clots. This may be important if: You have already had blood clots in your leg, arm, heart, or brain. Your health care provider is worried that a blood clot may form in your body. People who have a new heart valve, a large heart, a heart rhythm that is not normal, or other heart problems may need to take warfarin. When you are taking warfarin, you may be more likely to bleed, even from activities you have always done. Changing how you take your warfarin, taking other medicines, and eating certain foods all can change the way warfarin works in your body. If this happens, you may be more likely to form a clot or have bleeding problems. Taking Warfarin It is important that you take warfarin exactly as you have been told. Take only the dose your provider has prescribed. If you miss a dose, contact your provider for advice. If your pills look different from your last prescription, contact your provider or pharmacist right away. The tablets are different colors, depending on the dose. The dose is also marked on the pill. Your provider will test your blood at regular visits. This is called an INR test or sometimes a PT test. The test helps make sure you are taking the right amount of warfarin to help your body. Alcohol and some medicines can change how warfarin works in your body. DO NOT drink alcohol while you are taking warfarin. Talk with your provider before taking any other over-the-counter medicines, vitamins, supplements, cold medicines, antibiotics, or other medicines. Tell a...

Original English:

Heart attack â€“ discharge A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. This article discusses what you need to do to take care of yourself after you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You were in the hospital because you had a heart attack. A heart attack occurs when blood flow to a part of your heart is blocked long enough that part of the heart muscle is damaged or dies. What to Expect at Home You may feel sad. You may feel anxious and as though you have to be very careful about what you do. All of these feelings are normal. They go away for most people after 2 or 3 weeks. You may also feel tired when you leave the hospital to go home. Activity You should know the signs and symptoms of angina. You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in your chest. You may also notice these symptoms in your arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back. Some people also feel discomfort in their back, shoulders, and stomach area. You may have indigestion or feel sick to your stomach. You may feel tired and be short of breath, sweaty, lightheaded, or weak. You may have angina during physical activity, such as climbing stairs or walking uphill, lifting, sexual activity, or when you are out in cold weather. It can also happen when you are resting or it can wake you up when you are sleeping. Know how to treat your chest pain when it happens. Talk with your health care provider about what to do. Take it easy for the first 4 to 6 weeks after your heart attack. Avoid heavy lifting. Get some help with household chores if you can. Take 30 to 60 minutes to rest in the afternoon for first 4 to 6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get plenty of sleep. Before starting to exercise, your provider may have you do an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. This may happen before you leave the hospital or soon afterward. Heart attack (myocardial infarction) - Discharge Heart attack (myocardial infarction) occurs when blood flow to part of the heart is blocked for a long time, causing heart muscle to be damaged or necrotic. This article explains what you need to do to take care of yourself after being discharged from the hospital. When you were in the hospital You were hospitalized for heart attack (myocardial infarction). Heart attack occurs when blood flow to part of the heart is blocked for a long time, causing heart muscle to be damaged or necrotic. Expected things at home You may feel sad. You may feel worried and like you have to be very careful when doing anything. All these emotions are normal. They disappear after 2-3 weeks for most people. You may also feel fatigue when you are discharged from the hospital and go home. Activities You need to know the signs and symptoms of angina. - You may feel pressure, squeezing, burning, or tightness in the chest. You may feel these symptoms in the arms, shoulders, neck, jaw, throat, or back as well. - Some people feel discomfort in the back, shoulders, and abdomen. - You may have indigestion or feel heartburn. - You may feel tired, short of breath, sweaty, dizzy, and weak. - Angina can occur during physical activity such as climbing stairs, walking uphill, lifting heavy things, sexual activity, or going out in cold weather. It can also occur at rest and may wake you up while sleeping. - Know how to treat chest pain when it occurs. Consult with your healthcare provider about what to do. Do not overexert yourself for the first 4-6 weeks after a heart attack. - Do not lift heavy things. Get help from someone who helps with housework if possible. - Rest for 30-60 minutes in the afternoon for the first 4-6 weeks. Try to go to bed early and get enough sleep. - Before starting exercise, your healthcare provider may perform an exercise test and recommend an exercise plan. Item: EVAL_0319 | Document: CARD_004 | Model: Model D

Original English:

High blood pressure - what to ask your doctor When your heart pumps blood into your arteries, the pressure of the blood against the artery walls is called your blood pressure. Your blood pressure is given as two numbers: systolic over diastolic blood pressure. Your systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the course of your heart beat cycle. Your diastolic blood pressure is the lowest pressure. When your blood pressure gets too high, it puts extra stress on your heart and blood vessels. If your blood pressure stays high all the time, you will be at a higher risk for heart attacks and other vascular (blood vessel) diseases, strokes, kidney disease, and other health problems. Below are questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your blood pressure. Questions How can I change the way I live to lower my blood pressure? What is a heart-healthy diet? Is it OK to ever eat something that is not heart healthy? What are some ways to eat healthy when I go to a restaurant? Do I need to limit how much salt I use? Are there other spices that I can use to make my food taste good? Is it OK to drink alcohol? How much is OK? What can I do to stop smoking? Is it OK to be around other people who are smoking? Should I check my blood pressure at home? What type of equipment should I buy? Where can I learn how to use it? How often do I need to check my blood pressure? Should I write it down and bring it to my next visit? If I cannot check my own blood pressure, where else can I have it checked? What should my blood pressure reading be? Should I rest before taking my blood pressure? When should I contact my provider? What is my cholesterol? Do I need to take medicines for it? Is it OK to be sexually active? Is it safe to use sildenafil (Viagra), or tadalafil (Cialis), vardenafil (Staxyn), or avanafil (Stendra) for erection problems? What medicines am I taking to treat high blood pressure? Back-Translation (to English):

Hypertension - Things to Ask a Doctor When the heart pumps blood into the arteries, the pressure of blood against the artery walls is called blood pressure. Blood pressure is displayed as two numbers: systolic blood pressure/diastolic blood pressure. Systolic blood pressure is the highest blood pressure during the heart beat cycle. Diastolic blood pressure is the lowest blood pressure. When blood pressure becomes too high, it gives additional burden to the heart and blood vessels. When blood pressure continues to be maintained high, the risk of heart attack and other blood vessel (blood vessel) diseases, stroke, kidney disease, and other health problems becomes higher. The following are questions you may want to ask your healthcare provider for blood pressure management. How much drinking is okay? * What can I do to quit smoking? Is it okay to be around other people who smoke? Should I measure blood pressure at home? * What kind of equipment should I purchase? Where can I learn how to use it? * How often should I measure blood pressure? Should I record it and bring it to the next appointment? * If I cannot measure blood pressure myself, where can I get it measured? * What should my blood pressure numbers be? Should I rest before measuring blood pressure? * When should I contact my healthcare provider? What are my cholesterol numbers? Should I take medication? Is it okay to have a sex life? Item: EVAL_0341 | Document: DIAB_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Low blood sugar - self-care Low blood sugar is a condition that occurs when your blood sugar (glucose) is lower than normal. Low blood sugar may occur in people with diabetes who are taking insulin or certain other medicines to control their diabetes. Low blood sugar can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize the symptoms of low blood sugar and how to prevent them. What is Low Blood Sugar? Low blood sugar is called hypoglycemia. A blood sugar level below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L) is low and can harm you. A blood sugar level below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L) is a cause for immediate action. You are at risk for low blood sugar if you have diabetes and are taking any of the following diabetes medicines: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) You are also at increased risk of having low blood sugar if you have had previous low blood sugar levels. Recognizing Low Blood Sugar Know how to tell when your blood sugar is getting low. Symptoms include: Weakness or feeling tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling uneasy, nervous, or anxious Feeling cranky Trouble thinking clearly Double or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes your blood sugar may be too low even if you do not have symptoms. If it gets too low, you may: Faint Have a seizure Go into a coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time stop being able to sense low blood sugar. This is called hypoglycemic unawareness. Ask your health care provider if wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help you detect when your blood sugar is getting too low in order to help prevent symptoms. Check Your Blood Sugar Often Talk with your provider about when you should check your blood sugar every day. People who have low blood sugar need to check their blood sugar more often. The most common causes of low blood sugar are: Taking your insulin or diabetes medicine at the wrong time Taking too much insul...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hypoglycemia - Self-management Hypoglycemia is a condition where blood sugar (glucose) occurs lower than normal. Hypoglycemia can occur in diabetes patients who take insulin or certain other medications to manage diabetes. Hypoglycemia can cause dangerous symptoms. Learn how to recognize hypoglycemia symptoms and prevent them. What is hypoglycemia? Hypoglycemia is called hypoglycemia. If blood sugar level is below 70 mg/dL (3.9 mmol/L), it is hypoglycemia and can cause harm. If blood sugar level is below 54 mg/dL (3.0 mmol/L), immediate action is needed. Diabetes patients who take any one of the following diabetes medications are at risk for hypoglycemia: Insulin Glyburide (Micronase), glipizide (Glucotrol), glimepiride (Amaryl), repaglinide (Prandin), or nateglinide (Starlix) Even if you have had hypoglycemia before, the risk of hypoglycemia occurrence increases. Recognizing hypoglycemia You need to know how to notice that blood sugar is dropping. Symptoms include the following: Feeling weak or tired Shaking Sweating Headache Hunger Feeling anxious or nervous Feeling irritated Difficulty thinking clearly Overlapping or blurry vision Fast or pounding heartbeat Sometimes even if there are no symptoms, blood sugar can be too low. If blood sugar drops too low, the following can occur: Fainting Seizures Coma Some people who have had diabetes for a long time become unable to detect hypoglycemia. This is called hypoglycemia unawareness. Ask your health care provider whether wearing a continuous glucose monitor and sensor can help detect when blood sugar is dropping too low and help prevent symptoms. Check your blood sugar frequently Consult with your provider about when to check your blood sugar every day. People who have hypoglycemia need to check their blood sugar more frequently. The most common causes of hypoglycemia are as follows: Administering insulin or diabetes medication at the wrong time Administering too much insulin or diabetes medication Administer...

Original English:

Asthma - quick-relief drugs Asthma quick-relief medicines work fast to control asthma symptoms. You take them when you are coughing, wheezing, having trouble breathing, or having an asthma attack. They are also called rescue medicines. Many of these medicines are called "bronchodilators" because they open (dilate) and help relax the muscles of your airways (bronchi). You and your health care provider can make a plan for the quick-relief medicines that work for you. This plan will include when you should take them and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure you do not run out. Bring enough medicine with you when you travel. Short-acting Beta-agonists Short-acting beta-agonists are the most common quick-relief medicines for treating asthma attacks and are considered to be bronchodilators. They can be used just before exercising to help prevent asthma symptoms caused by exercise. They work by relaxing the muscles of your airways, and this lets you breathe better during an attack. Tell your provider if you are using quick-relief medicines twice a week or more to control your asthma symptoms. Your asthma may not be under control, and your provider may need to change your dose of daily control medicines. Some quick-relief asthma medicines include: Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) Metaproterenol Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonists may cause these side effects: Anxiety. Fast and irregular heartbeats. Call your provider right away if you have this side effect. Headache. Restlessness. Tremor (your hand or another part of your body may shake). Oral Steroids Your provider might prescribe oral steroids when you have an asthma attack that is not going away. These are medicines that you take by mouth as pills, capsules, or liquids. Oral steroids are not quick-relief medicines but are often given for 7 to 14 days when your symptoms flare-up. Oral steroids include: Methylprednisolone Prednisone Prednisolone

Back-Translation (to English):

Asthma - Medications that quickly relieve symptoms Asthma rapid relief medication quickly controls asthma symptoms. Take when there is cough, wheezing, breathing difficulty, or asthma attack. This medication is also called rescue medication. Many of these medications are called "bronchodilators" because they relax and open airway (bronchus) muscles. You and your healthcare provider can make a plan for effective rapid relief medication for you. This plan includes when you should take it and how much you should take. Plan ahead. Make sure medicine doesn't run out. Take enough medicine when traveling. Short-acting beta-agonist Short-acting beta-agonist is the most commonly used rapid relief medication for asthma attack treatment and is considered a bronchodilator. This medication can help prevent exercise-induced asthma symptoms when used before exercise. It works by relaxing airway muscles, which enables better breathing during attacks. If you are using rapid relief medication more than twice a week to control asthma symptoms, inform your healthcare provider. Asthma may not be properly controlled, and your healthcare provider may need to change the dosage of daily control medication. Some asthma rapid relief medications are as follows: - Albuterol (ProAir HFA, Proventil HFA, Ventolin HFA) - Levalbuterol (Xopenex HFA) - Metaproterenol - Terbutaline Short-acting beta-agonist can cause the following side effects: - Anxiety. - Fast and irregular heartbeat. If this side effect appears, call your healthcare provider immediately. - Headache. - Restlessness. - Tremor (hands or other parts of body may tremble). Oral steroids Your healthcare provider can prescribe oral steroids when asthma attack doesn't go away. This medication is medication taken through mouth in pill, capsule, or liquid form. Oral steroids are not rapid relief medication but are often administered for 7 to 14 days when symptoms worsen. Oral steroids are as follows: - Methylprednisolone - Pre...

COPD - what to ask your doctor Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease (COPD) damages your lungs. This can make it hard for you to get enough oxygen and clear carbon dioxide from your lungs. While there is no cure for COPD, you can do many things to regulate your symptoms and make your life better. Below are some questions you may want to ask your health care provider to help you take care of your lungs. Questions What will make my COPD worse? How can I avoid things that can make my COPD worse? Which medicines should I take when I am short of breath (called quick-relief or rescue medicines)? Is it OK to use these medicines every day? What are the side effects of my medicines? For what side effects should I contact my provider? Am I using my inhaler the right way? Should I be using a spacer? How will I know when my inhalers are getting empty? When should I use my nebulizer and when should I use my inhaler? What sort of changes should I make around my home? What sort of changes do I need to make at work? Will I need oxygen on the airplane? How about at the airport? What medicines should I bring? Who should I contact if my COPD gets worse? Original English:

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin-converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are medicines. They treat heart, blood vessel, and kidney problems. How ACE inhibitors help ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These medicines make your heart work less hard by lowering your blood pressure. This keeps some kinds of heart disease from getting worse. Most people who have heart failure take one of these medicines or similar medicines. These medicines treat high blood pressure, strokes, or heart attacks. They may help lower your risk for stroke or heart attack. They are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help keep your kidneys from getting worse or even improve the function of your kidneys, especially if you have diabetes. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider if you should be taking these medicines. Types of ACE inhibitors There are many different names and brands of ACE inhibitors. Most work as well as another. Side effects may be different for different ones. Taking Your ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills that you take by mouth. Take all of your medicines as your provider told you to. Follow up with your provider regularly. Your provider will check your blood pressure and do blood tests to make sure the medicines are working properly. Your provider may change your dose from time to time. In addition: Try to take your medicines at the same time each day. Don't stop taking your medicines without talking to your provider first. Plan ahead so that you do not run out of medicine. Make sure you have enough with you when you travel. Before taking ibuprofen (Advil, Motrin) or aspirin, talk to your provider. Tell your provider what other medicines you are taking, including anything you bought without a prescription, diuretics (water pills), potassium pills, or herbal or dietary supplements. Don't take ACE inhibitors if you are planning to become pregnant, are pregnant, or are breastfeeding. Contact your provider promptly...

Back-Translation (to English):

ACE inhibitors Angiotensin converting enzyme (ACE) inhibitors are drugs. These drugs treat heart, blood vessel, kidney problems. Help of ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are used to treat heart disease and high blood pressure. These drugs lower blood pressure so the heart works less hard. This prevents some heart diseases from worsening. Most people with heart failure take these drugs or similar drugs. These drugs treat high blood pressure, stroke, heart attack. These drugs can help lower the risk of stroke or heart attack. These drugs are also used to treat kidney problems. This can help prevent kidney function from worsening, or improve kidney function especially if diabetes is present. If you have these problems, ask your health care provider whether you should take these drugs. Types of ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors have several different names and brands. Most drugs have similar effects to each other. Side effects may differ depending on the drug. How to take ACE inhibitors ACE inhibitors are pills taken by mouth. Take all medications as directed by your provider. Visit your provider regularly for follow-up management. Your provider will check blood pressure and perform blood tests to confirm the medication is working properly. Your provider may sometimes change the dosage. Also: 1. Take the medication at the same time every day as much as possible. 2. Do not stop taking the medication without consulting your provider. 3. Plan ahead so you don't run out of medication. Inform your provider of other medications you are taking, including over-the-counter medications, diuretics (water pills), potassium supplements, herbal products or nutritional supplements. 6. Stroke - Discharge You were hospitalized after suffering a stroke. Stroke occurs when blood flow to part of the brain stops. At home, follow the self-care instructions of your healthcare provider. Use the information below for review purposes. **When you were in the hospital** First, you received treatment to prevent additional damage to the brain and to allow the heart, lungs, and other important organs to recover. After your condition stabilized, your healthcare provider performed tests and started treatment to recover from the stroke and prevent future strokes. You may have stayed in a special ward that helps with recovery after stroke. **What to expect at home** Due to brain damage caused by stroke, the following problems may appear: * Behavioral changes * Performing simple tasks * Memory * Movement of one side of the body * Muscle spasms * Concentration * Sensation or cognition of parts of the body * Swallowing * Speaking or understanding others * Thinking * One-sided vision (hemianopsia) You may need help with daily activities you did alone before the stroke. You may receive physical therapy or occupational therapy to recover these functions. Depression is quite common while living and adapting to changes after stroke. It can develop immediately after stroke or up to 2 years after stroke. Do not drive a car without permission from your healthcare provider. **Moving** Moving and doing daily tasks may be difficult after stroke. Make sure your home is safe. Ask your healthcare provider, therapist, or nurse about how to modify your home to make it easier to perform daily activities. Find out ways to prevent falls and to use the bathroom safely. Family and caregivers need to help with the following: * Exercises to keep elbows, shoulders, and other joints flexible * Monitoring joint stiffness (contracture) * Making sure assistive devices are used correctly * Making sure limbs maintain good posture when sitting or lying ...

Item: EVAL_0375 | Document: SURG_001 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Surgical wound care - open An incision is a cut through the skin that is made during surgery. It is also called a surgical wound. Some incisions are small, others are long. The size of the incision depends on the kind of surgery you had. Sometimes, an incision breaks open. This may happen along the entire cut or just part of it. Your surgeon may decide not to close it again with sutures (stitches). What to Expect at Home If your surgeon does not close your wound again with sutures, you need to care for it at home, since it may take time to heal. The wound will heal from the bottom to the top. A dressing helps absorb drainage and keep the skin from closing before the wound underneath fills in. Proper Handwashing It is important to clean your hands before you change your dressing. You can use an alcohol-based cleanser. Or, you can wash your hands using these steps: Take all jewelry off your hands. Wet your hands, pointing them downward under warm running water. Add soap and wash your hands for 15 to 30 seconds (sing "Happy Birthday" or the "Alphabet Song" one time through). Clean under your nails also. Rinse well. Dry with a clean towel. Removing the Old Dressing Your health care provider will tell you how often to change your dressing. To prepare for the dressing change: Clean your hands before touching the dressing. Make sure you have all the supplies handy. Have a clean work surface. Remove the old dressing: Carefully loosen the tape from your skin. Use a clean (not sterile) medical glove to grab the old dressing and pull it off. If the dressing sticks to the wound, wet it and try again, unless your provider instructed you to pull it off dry. Put the old dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside. Clean your hands again after you take off the old dressing. Caring for the Wound You may use a gauze pad or soft cloth to clean the skin around your wound: Use a normal saline solution (salt water) or mild soapy water. Soak the gauze or cloth in the saline solutio...

Back-Translation (to English):

--- --- Surgical wound management - Open type An incision is cutting the skin during surgery. This is also called a surgical wound. There are cases where the incision is small and cases where it is long. The size of the incision varies depending on the type of surgery you have received. Sometimes the incision site can open or split. This can occur across the entire incision or only in part. The surgeon in charge may decide not to close it again with sutures (thread). Expected situation at home If the surgeon does not close the wound again with sutures, recovery may take time, so you must manage it directly at home. The wound heals from bottom to top. Dressing helps absorb drainage and prevent the skin from closing before the bottom of the wound is filled. Proper hand washing It is important to wash hands cleanly before replacing dressing. You can use alcohol-based hand sanitizer. Or you can wash your hands according to the following steps: 1. Remove all jewelry from your hands. 2. With hands facing down, wet your hands under warm running water. 3. Apply soap and wash your hands for 15-30 seconds (while singing the Happy Birthday song or ABC song once). Clean under your fingernails as well. 4. Rinse cleanly. 5. Dry with a clean towel. Removing used dressing Medical staff will tell you how often you should replace dressing. To prepare for dressing replacement: - Wash your hands cleanly before touching dressing. - Prepare so that all necessary items can be easily obtained. - Prepare a clean work surface. Remove the used dressing: - Carefully peel off the tape from the skin. - Using clean (not sterile) medical gloves, grasp and remove the used dressing. - If the dressing is stuck to the wound, wet it with water and try again unless medical staff instructed you to peel it off dry. - Put the used dressing in a plastic bag and set it aside on one side. - Wash your hands cleanly again after removing the used dressing. Item: EVAL_0383 | Document: SURG_002 | Model: Model D

Original English:

Hip replacement - discharge You had surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint called a prosthesis. This article tells you what you need to do to care for your new hip when you leave the hospital. When You're in the Hospital You had a hip joint replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. What to Expect at Home By the time you go home, you should be able to walk with a walker or crutches without needing much help. Most people do not need them after 2 to 4 weeks. Check with your surgeon about when to stop using crutches. You should also be able to dress yourself with only a little help and be able to get into and out of your bed or a chair by yourself. You should also be able to use the toilet without much help. You will need to be careful that you do not dislocate your artificial hip, especially in the first few months after surgery. You will need to learn exercises that make your new hip stronger and take special precautions. You will need to have someone with you at home for 1 to 2 weeks after you leave the hospital or rehab center. You will need help preparing meals, bathing, moving around the house, and doing other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your former level of activity. You will need to avoid some sports, such as downhill skiing or contact sports like football and soccer. But you should be able to do low impact activities, such as hiking, gardening, swimming, playing tennis, and golfing. Home Setup Your bed should be low enough for your feet to touch the floor when you sit on the edge of the bed. Your bed should also be high enough so that your hips are higher than your knees when you sit on the edge. You may not need a hospital bed, but your mattress should be firm. Keep tripping hazards out of your home. Learn to prevent falls. Remove loose wires or cords from areas you walk through to get from one...

Back-Translation (to English):

Hip Replacement Surgery - Discharge You have undergone surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis (prosthesis). This document provides guidance on how to care for your new hip joint after discharge. While in the hospital You have undergone hip replacement surgery to replace all or part of your hip joint with an artificial joint. This artificial joint is called a prosthesis. Expected situation at home By the time of discharge, you should be able to walk with almost no help using a walker or crutches. Most people will not need walking assistive devices after 2-4 weeks. Consult your surgeon about when to discontinue crutch use. Additionally, you should be able to dress yourself with only a little help, and get in and out of bed or chairs by yourself. You should also be able to use the bathroom by yourself with almost no help. Especially during the first few months after surgery, you must be careful to prevent dislocation of the artificial hip joint. You must learn exercises to make the new hip joint stronger and follow special precautions. For 1-2 weeks after leaving the hospital or rehabilitation center, you should have someone with you at home. You will need help with meal preparation, bathing, moving around the house, and other daily activities. Over time, you should be able to return to your previous activity level. You should avoid contact sports like alpine skiing, American football, or soccer. However, you should be able to do low-impact activities like hiking, gardening, swimming, tennis, and golf. Preparing your home environment The bed should be low enough that your feet touch the floor when you sit on it. Additionally, it should be high enough that your hips are positioned higher than your knees when you sit on the bed. A hospital bed is not absolutely necessary, but the mattress should be firm. Remove fall hazards in your home. Prevent falls. â€¢ Remove loose electrical cor...
